Municipal Bureau of Education Establishes Compulsory Education Committee; Appoints Tong Xingbai, et al., as Members; and Formulates Regulations for the Committee

In order to implement compulsory education in this city, the Municipal Bureau of Education has specially established a Compulsory Education Committee and formulated the Organizational Regulations of the Shanghai Municipal Compulsory Education Committee, appointing Tong Xingbai, Tao Baichuan, Zhu Fengwei, Hong Kui, Chen Bai, Du Gang, Sheng Zhensheng, Tai Shuangqiu, Tao Xingzhi, Dong Renjian, representatives of the Finance Bureau, and representatives of the Public Security Bureau as members. The organizational regulations of the committee are recorded as follows: Article 1: In order to implement compulsory education in this city, the Shanghai Municipal Bureau of Education, in accordance with the provisions of Chapter 7 of the outline of the provisional measures for the implementation of compulsory education promulgated by the Ministry of Education, organizes the Shanghai Municipal Compulsory Education Committee (hereinafter referred to as "this committee"). Article 2: This committee shall have nine to fifteen members, with the director of the Education Bureau serving as the chairman, and the following personnel shall be invited or designated as members: (1) the head of the relevant department of the Education Bureau; (2) the supervisor of the Education Bureau; (3) a representative of the Municipal Government Secretariat; (4) a representative of the Finance Bureau; (5) a representative of the Public Security Bureau; (6) persons of high standing in the education community. Article 3: When this committee holds a meeting, the chairman shall preside. In the absence of the chairman, one of the members shall be designated as the acting chairman. Article 4: The main tasks of this committee are as follows: 1. To formulate a plan for the implementation of compulsory education throughout the city; 2. To supervise the safekeeping and expenditure of compulsory education funds in this city and the subsidies granted by the central government for compulsory education in this city; 3. To review the budget and final accounts of the affiliated compulsory education funds; 4. To formulate measures for the annual training of teachers; 5. To assess the performance of the affiliated compulsory education institutions; 6. Other matters related to the implementation of compulsory education. Article 5: All members of this committee shall serve in name only, but appointed members shall be reimbursed for travel expenses depending on the distance of their travel. Article 6: This committee shall have one secretary-general and several staff members, who shall be transferred from the staff of the Education Bureau by the chairman. Article 7: Detailed rules for the operation of this committee shall be formulated separately. Article 8: These regulations shall be implemented after being submitted to the Ministry of Education and the Municipal Government for approval and record.

The Current Status and Plan of Compulsory Education in Jiangsu

— (Report by Zhou Fo-hai at the Jiangsu Provincial Government Memorial Week)

According to simple statistics for Jiangsu's compulsory education in the 19th year of the Republic (August 1930 to July 1931), there were only over 7,350 primary schools, over 12,700 elementary levels, and only over 580,000 enrolled children. There were approximately over 3.9 million children who were not enrolled. The percentage of enrolled children was only about 12.6%. When I took office, I witnessed such a situation and believed that it was imperative to find a way to provide relief quickly. At the same time, because the educational funds of the counties had been arbitrarily and abusively used for many years, resulting in extreme disorder and mostly deficits, it was naturally impossible to expand the undertaking further. Therefore, for the work of promoting compulsory education, only the following measures were decided: (1) increasing enrollment; (2) improving private schools; (3) encouraging private individuals to establish schools; (4) stipulating that primary schools should be established in townships. Although the ideal goal has not been achieved in the past four years, according to concise statistical figures, it cannot be said that there has been no progress. According to the statistics for the 22nd year of the Republic, the number of schools has increased to 9,053, the number of levels has increased to 16,682, and the number of enrolled children has increased to 800,489. The percentage of enrolled children has also increased to 14.8%. This slight progress, although a drop in the bucket and of no help in alleviating the difficulties of the time, in a time of declining people's livelihoods and the prevalence of natural disasters, this situation has made me feel mentally and physically exhausted.

Between February and March of this year, I felt that although Zhenjiang was the provincial capital, the number of out-of-school children was even higher than in the counties around Suzhou, Changzhou, Shanghai, and Haifeng, and it was deemed necessary to provide relief urgently. Therefore, it was planned to allocate a portion of the provincial education funds to establish several compulsory education schools in the urban area of the provincial capital as a partial remedy. Coincidentally, the Ministry of Education also had plans to subsidize the management of compulsory education. Upon receiving this news, this department sent personnel to the Ministry of Education for multiple consultations, requesting more funding to facilitate progress. Later, the Ministry of Education telegraphed to require that at least half of the funds be raised locally in order to be eligible for the supplementary funds. Minister Wang of the Ministry of Education personally came to Zhenjiang and, together with Chairman Chen and myself, jointly agreed on the amount of funding to be allocated. As a result, it was decided that the province would raise 150,000 yuan, and the central government would also allocate 150,000 yuan. Currently, the province has decided to allocate 100,000 yuan from the provincial education fund's overseas study fee item and 50,000 yuan from the provincial treasury. The central government has already telegraphed its agreement to provide 120,000 yuan. After sending personnel to the ministry to vigorously advocate, it is estimated that another 30,000 yuan can be allocated from the Boxer Indemnity funds to make up 150,000 yuan.

Now that the funds have been secured, it is necessary to formulate a plan to prepare for implementation. In the past two weeks, a provincial compulsory education committee meeting was held, and the proposed implementation plan was passed. The content of the plan can be divided into several aspects:

(1) Source of Funding

(1) The province will raise 150,000 yuan, and the central government will subsidize 150,000 yuan, for a total of 300,000 yuan.

(2) The counties will raise or apportion the start-up costs locally, including equipment and school building repairs, estimated at approximately 300,000 yuan. 50 of the 61 counties must raise half of the operating expenses themselves. This amount will be used from the county's general reserve fund or the education endowment fund, totaling approximately 110,000 yuan. The total of items 1 and 2 reaches over 700,000 yuan.

(2) Implementation Methods

(A) Number of Levels to Be Established (excluding Jiangning Autonomous Experimental County): A. 100 levels in the urban area of the provincial capital; B. 50 levels each in Lishui, Gaochun, Jiangpu, Liuhe, Yangzhou, Yizheng, Jurong, Donghai, Huaiyin, and Guanyun counties, totaling 500 levels. If the full start-up costs cannot be raised immediately, they can be established in spring and autumn; C. 10 to 15 levels each in the other 50 counties, totaling 750 levels.

A total of 1,350 levels will be added.

(B) Content of Management: A. At least half of the newly established levels should be one-year short-term primary schools and must adopt a two-shift system; B. The regular elementary school levels in the newly established levels should also adopt a two-shift system; C. The number of students per level in the newly established levels must be at least 50, taught by one teacher. However, in principle, each teacher in the two-shift system should teach 100 students; D. The locations for establishing levels in each county should prioritize areas with more out-of-school children. The number of enrolled children added is estimated at 700 levels with a two-shift system, with 100 students per level, totaling 70,000 students; 650 levels with 50 students per level, totaling 32,500 students, for a total of 102,500 students.

(3) Teachers

(1) Teachers in the urban area of the provincial capital: The provincial normal schools and rural normal schools have been ordered to recommend outstanding graduates of this year to be selected; this is sufficient. (2) Lishui and other ten counties: Because many levels are being established, there is a shortage of teachers. In addition to prioritizing graduates of provincial and county normal schools, teachers for the levels established in the spring can be filled by high school graduates who have been trained for three months. (3) Other counties will mainly employ graduates of provincial and county normal schools or recruit through examinations.

(4) Allocation of Funds

(1) A total of 750 newly added levels in each county at 150 yuan (half) each, totaling 112,500 yuan; (2) A total of 500 levels in Lishui and other ten counties at 300 yuan each, totaling 150,000 yuan; (3) A total of 100 levels in the provincial capital at 250 yuan each, totaling 35,000 yuan; (4) Reserve fund of 2,500 yuan, totaling 300,000 yuan.

(5) School Buildings and Equipment

(1) School buildings: Utilize public venues, or build simple sheds, or rent private houses; (2) Equipment should be as simple as possible; students can bring their own desks and chairs. The content of the plan is as described above. The number of levels to be established in each county has already been notified for compliance, and a deadline has been set for submitting plans for review. In the provincial capital, a preparatory committee will be established separately, inviting enthusiastic local gentlemen to assist jointly. The first step, investigating the number of school-age children and the number of out-of-school children, has already been investigated and clarified. There are approximately 8,367 out-of-school children in the urban area, of which 3,584 children aged 9 to 12 can enter short-term primary schools. There are 56 usable school buildings, such as public offices, ancestral halls, and temples, which can accommodate about 60 levels. The insufficient number still requires the construction of sheds or the rental of private houses. Regarding the progress steps, a preparatory committee meeting was also held, and it was resolved that Mr. Leng Yuhui, Mr. Lu Xiaobo, and Mr. Yan Huiyu would be responsible for borrowing 15,000 yuan for start-up costs. Regarding the determination of school locations, planning of school building equipment, solicitation of private donations of school equipment and teaching materials, appointment of principals, and selection of teachers, there have been proposals and decisions. Regarding compulsory enrollment methods, steps have also been discussed, namely, first, persuasion; second, posting names for warning; and third, fines of one to five yuan. We are now proceeding step by step according to the plan.

This time, the central government and the province are using all their strength to focus on the implementation and promotion of compulsory education, and I believe that considerable results will be achieved. However, after several months of preparation, I feel that the most difficult thing is still the funding. Fortunately, according to the above plan, the operating expenses are settled. The remaining problems are only the start-up costs and the problem of school buildings and school equipment. The start-up costs are stipulated to be raised or apportioned. Although this seems to increase the burden on the people, the start-up costs only need to be paid once and then it is finished, unlike the operating expenses that need to be borne every year. Moreover, paying once makes it more convenient for the children to go to school with one more primary school in the local area, so I hope that local people will actively donate. It is estimated that 100 yuan is enough for the start-up costs per level. If each family contributes one yuan, 100 families can raise 100 yuan, and the burden cannot be considered heavy. As for school buildings and school equipment, I hope that local people will, on the one hand, lend ancestral halls and temples as school buildings, and on the other hand, donate unnecessary tables and chairs from their homes. We can then work hard in a simple and economical manner. If our comrades in the party and government agencies can vigorously advocate and promote, I think it will definitely be promoted in the society. I hope everyone will work hard! (End)

The Shanghai Municipal Bureau of Education hereby announces to all private schools that, if they need to request subsidy fees for the 24th year of the Republic, they should fill out a form and submit it to the bureau for review. Municipal Bureau of Education Issues Order to Adopt World Primary and Secondary School Textbooks

In order to meet educational needs, the Ministry of Education has formulated new curriculum standards for primary and secondary schools. The World Book Company has compiled textbooks for various subjects in primary and secondary schools according to these standards. Schools at all levels, including senior high schools, junior high schools, and primary schools, are fully equipped and have been reviewed and approved by the Ministry of Education. Because the preparation materials are very suitable for the needs, all schools are vying to adopt them. Recently, the Ministry of Education found that a few schools were using unapproved and outdated textbooks, which is inconsistent with the established educational standards. An order has been issued to the whole country that all school textbooks must uniformly adopt the approved version. Little Boy (10) (3)

— Original work by American Ms. Alcott, translated by Zheng Xiaocang (Zonghai)

Then she tried to get him to tell her all his sufferings. Her own eyes were filled with tears as she listened to the little story, although it was not a new story to her.

"My child, you now have a father and a mother. This is your home. Don't think about those sad situations anymore. Just try to get better and be happy. You will definitely not suffer anymore. We will definitely do our best for you. This place was originally set up for all kinds of children, to let them have happy days here and gradually be able to stand on their own and become useful people, I hope. You can play music as much as you like, but you must first strengthen your body. Now go to Natto, take a bath, and then go to bed. Tomorrow we can jointly decide on some interesting little plans."

Ned held her hand tightly, without saying a word, only letting his grateful eyes speak for him. At the same time, Mrs. Pei led him to a large room, where they saw a strong German woman with a round, fat face and a cheerful expression like a sun. The edge of her small hat was exactly like the radiation of sunlight.

"This is Hu Nansui, she will give you a refreshing bath, cut your hair, and make you feel comfortable. That's the bathroom. On Saturday evenings, we first wipe down the smaller children together, and while the older children are still singing, we put them to bed first. Now, Robert, go in first."

As Mrs. Pei spoke, she had already taken off Robert's clothes and pushed him into a long tub in a small room that was connected to the nursery.

Oil Market: Sales of Asphalt in Hankou are Brisk, and Prices are Rising – Local Market Sellers Also Agree to Increase Prices –

Asphalt sales are currently strong in the autumn season. Merchants in various places need to purchase various brands of goods to meet the demand for candles. Therefore, Hankou is the central market. However, recently, there have been cases of ships directly transporting goods from Yichang to Shanghai for sale. Currently, the inventory in Hankou is thinner than in previous years. Therefore, whenever there is a purchase, prices rise. Recently, the Hankou, Zhenning, Suzhou, and Shanghai gangs all want to purchase, but the Hankou merchants do not have enough to supply. The existing goods must be negotiated with the sellers. The sellers, seeing that there are more buyers, hoard and raise prices. Chongjing oil has increased from 12 taels per "chu" [unit of weight] to 13 taels and 6 qian. The lowest price for Macheng oil was 11 taels and 4 qian, and it has now increased to 12 taels, 8 qian, and 7 fen. The price increase is due to the scramble for purchases by various gangs. However, these are all for reserves and not due to genuine sales. Therefore, seeing that the prices have risen so much, buyers are hesitant and waiting for an opportunity. However, once the prices have risen, they will not easily fall. Local merchants, seeing the large price increase in Hankou, have also agreed to increase their selling prices, opening at 22 yuan and 24 jiao per chu for Chongjing oil, 22 yuan to 21 yuan and 68 jiao for Pingjing oil, and 19 yuan per city scale for Macheng oil.

Free Talk

Art for Art's Sake and Art for Life's Sake

— Xu Maoyong

"Art for art's sake," which only emphasizes the "beauty of technique" in pure literature, not only seems theoretically impure but also has little standing in reality. Universally recognized great literary works throughout history do not contain such empty concepts; this is beyond doubt. Furthermore, the story of Oscar Wilde mentioned in André Gide's memoirs also reveals some information.

Oscar Wilde was a proponent of aestheticism, advocating that art should be detached from life and transcend it. He even more extremely praised pleasure and lamented the decadence of "lying," saying, "All bad art comes from returning to nature and life." However, when he entered prison for the sake of aesthetic living and saw the reality of life, his aestheticism shattered. He admitted that his previous heart, which only pursued beauty and pleasure, was "a heart like a stubborn stone," and this "heart like a stubborn stone" was broken by something called "compassion." When discussing Dostoevsky's works, he said the following:

"Russian writers are outstanding. What makes their works so great is the compassion they embody. I used to love reading 'Madame Bovary,' but Flaubert was unwilling to put compassion into his work, so his work seems petty and narrow; compassion makes the work expansive and great." (See Wilde's "The Critic as Artist," translated in the second issue of the second volume)

Shifting his love from Flaubert to Russian writers and advocating for compassion in works signifies that the aestheticism of "art for art's sake" ultimately succumbed to "art for life's sake."

When he favored Flaubert, Wilde opposed Zola. In his decadent "lying," he said:

"From an artistic point of view, Zola's works are almost worthless, deserving only zero points. The characters in his works are decadent and pitiful people, the crystallization of sin. Their lives are records without interest. Who can pay attention to these people? Our demand for art is rarity, charm, imagination, and simply, beauty."

The difference between Zola and Flaubert is precisely the difference between "art for life's sake" and "art for art's sake." Although Flaubert's realism does not deviate from the reality of life, he also said, "Life is empty, art is everything." He regarded art itself as the only absolute, while life was merely a model to be depicted. At the root of Zola's naturalistic writing attitude was compassion for life, so he had an idealistic desire to transform society. In this regard, Zola was closer to Russian writers than Flaubert. It was natural that Wilde opposed Zola when he favored Flaubert. But when he recognized compassion and disliked Flaubert's pettiness and narrowness, he would also have to change his attitude towards Zola and retract the above criticism—this was also natural.

The presence or absence of compassion is the fundamental distinction between "art for life's sake" and "art for art's sake," i.e., pure literature.

Mass literature, of course, should first be "art for life's sake"; it must have compassion. However, it cannot be said that all "art for life's sake" is mass literature.

For example, art that depicts the poor, the oppressed, and the starving is "art for life's sake" that embodies compassion. There have already been many such arts. Since the naturalistic movement, there have been countless works depicting workers and farmers. However, it cannot be said to be mass literature simply because it depicts workers and farmers. Why is this? It is because the author uses the eyes of an outsider or a privileged person to observe and depict, and because the author lacks a common consciousness or demand with the masses themselves.

For example, works that merely complain about the harshness of the living environment and express endless dissatisfaction and frustration are also "art for life's sake" that embodies compassion. However, the author cannot point out a positive attitude towards reality, the cultivation of labor and will, a new way of life, a constructive enthusiasm, and hatred for those bad inheritances—the old world—that are overcome by our difficulties and sufferings.

For example, depicting human psychology, exploring the secrets of the soul, showing the victory of human barbarity and selfish instincts, or pointing out the goodness of human nature is also "art for life's sake" that embodies negative or positive compassion. However, it cannot be said to be mass literature either. Why? Because the author only depicts individual psychology with a religious, ethical, or philosophical attitude but ignores the social and historical factors that determine all individual psychology, and this is its shortcoming.

Furthermore, pure literature, because it only seeks the "beauty of technique," specializes in carving and affectation, often using exaggerated metaphors or strange ancient language to make it difficult for readers to understand. And "art for art's sake" pure literature wants to transcend the real world and is satisfied with simply depicting empty beauty with technique. Naturalistic literature of "art for life's sake" explains, describes, and tastes everything in the world. Mass literature has its ideals. This is the most important point of distinction.

Sparrows and Stove Bugs

— Yu

Those engaged in literature and art have long been deemed less useful than shoemakers, butchers, and barbers. Perhaps precisely because they are useless, society's treatment of them is meager, as can be imagined. People are not sparrows, but they have to live like sparrows on the roof. People are not stove bugs, but they have to gnaw on crumbs of bread crusts like stove bugs. If there is always a roof to live under and always crumbs of bread crusts to gnaw on, their attention may not be focused solely on this. But we must know that the fact is that today there is a roof to live under, but whether there will be one tomorrow is uncertain, or whether there is a roof to live under this month, but whether there will be one next month is uncertain. Whether there are crumbs of bread crusts to gnaw on today, and whether there will be any to gnaw on tomorrow, or whether there are bread crusts to gnaw on this month, but whether there will be any to gnaw on next month, is a serious and not insignificant problem for them. It is a slander against them to say that those engaged in literature and art lack artistic conscience. All artists are loyal to their art and strive to improve. They have ambition, and like Kleist, they want to snatch the poet's laurel from others' heads and put it on their own. But in order to plan for tomorrow's bread and housing, they have to write more and exchange it for meager稿費. This is due to the pressure of hunger and is not necessarily of their own free will. Squeezed-out bitter juice and sweat are only sought for sales, and naturally, there is no time to care about "curved" or "straight," "elegance" or "beauty." This is a matter for those few leisured artists who live well, and they have no time to care about it, nor do they have the economic ability to care about such things. Because they live in sparrow-like roofs, the air they breathe is polluted, and they cannot even see a patch of green grass, let alone a花圍別墅. The dirty alleys and the ragged and filthy characters that come out of them mean that inspiration cannot come from the outside, and the fire of hunger burns out from the inside. Picking up a pen to write is writing for a purpose, which fundamentally loses the condition of "leisure." Moreover, there are no cigars to smoke, no coffee to drink, and even a cup of bitter tea cannot be obtained. However, they can only write, write, and write endlessly until their hands cannot move. Shaking their hands is considered stretching their muscles, and looking outward is considered letting in air. Looking at the瓦 on the opposite side is considered relaxing. That's all. Articles written in this environment naturally do not have noble ladies as typical characters, nor do they have magnificent natural backgrounds as objects of description, nor do they have ancient books that speak of the soul as 引襯. Therefore, those "leisurely and elegant" artists who are full of ancient interest will have headaches when they see such articles and will find them uninteresting, thinking that Chinese magazines and newspapers are not worth reading and that only picture magazines for公子哥儿 and leisured people suit their tastes.

Could Be Currie (Part 2)

— Zhang Yiping

That old man, Lian Lao, hurriedly pulled at his face, bending his waist a few times with a flattering smile, retreating to the table next to him with the enthusiasm of A Geng.

A Geng, naturally, was again reaching out to the shelf to get his own water bottle. As he put the tea leaves in, he secretly glanced this way. A Geng was clever at making money; he wanted to get back the "Daidaihua" tea that he had put in Master Shi's teapot from these two gentlemen. And he knew that even if they could taste the tea, they wouldn't dare to make a fuss in front of Director Shi.

They started talking. Without listening, A Geng knew what it was about: first, a while about the weather, how the sunshine and rain made people dizzy, and then about the rice harvest—they were both big landlords in Wujia Town, and these were the things they cared about most. They would never misreport the price挂牌 on the market by even a cent. A Geng never quite believed their methods and would go to the market after closing time to ask his friends, trying to figure out the connection—they never made a mistake. In the end, he'd pound his head, thinking that his own brain must be wired wrong.

He put the teapot on the two gentlemen's table, refilled Master Shi's cup, and turned around to look at the sun, which had already shone inside the threshold. Eight o'clock, thought A Geng, maybe he should remind Master Shi if he wanted a shaobing.

But they were talking enthusiastically. After the rice market, Old Mu talked about the return of the eldest son of the Lu family. He had gotten fat, wore foreign clothes, and walked with great authority. "And I heard he brought back two big trunks!" Old Mu also had Director Shi's trick, lowering his voice to a buzz like a fly over a manure pit. "His father specially invited me to dinner last night."

Normally, Director Shi would have jumped up. Who did Old Mu think he was? How dare the Lu family not invite me, Director Shi, to dinner! But Director Shi was completely different today. He smiled affably and said, "I heard it yesterday but didn't believe it. I wonder what the boy is like now?"

"Director, it's really unbelievable!" Lian Lao chimed in. He was also a guest of the Lu family, and feeling that Old Mu hadn't mentioned his visit was a slight to his face, he deliberately raised his voice half an octave. "Lu Quanfu insisted on dragging me along yesterday too. Ah Xing has changed completely after wandering around outside. He's very polite. At the dinner table, he talked about Shanghai, Hankou, Nanjing, all the many places he's been. He said he was doing business that had advantages of tens of thousands of silver taels, that's the small age, it's a loss for him..." Lian Lao blurted out, and suddenly felt like someone had punched him in the waist; Old Mu was winking at him to keep quiet. Only then did he see that Director Shi's eyes were as ugly as if his stomach was on fire. For a moment, he didn't know how to wind down and could only say "Yes, yes, yes" for a long time, ending with "That boy also sent his regards to the Director."

Although Lian Lao was anxious, he thought his lie was very clever.

In fact, Director Shi hadn't listened to everything Lian Lao said. After he confirmed that Lu Ah Xing had returned, his entire brain was turning over what A Geng couldn't have guessed as a "connection." It was Director Shi's masterpiece from back then: when he wanted to swallow a piece of land from Lu Quanfu, that little brat Ah Xing dared to try to topple me, Director Shi (he naturally wasn't a Director back then), a local tyrant and evil gentry or something, and almost tripped him up by a child. But Shi was not so easily stumped, his inch-long fingernails had rhythmically knocked on the table several times, he was already a glorious Director Shi, and Ah Xing's name was mixed in with those of xxx, scaring him into running away overnight. Director Shi never tired of telling his wife about this affair ten or a hundred times in exhaustive detail when he was tipsy, stroking his unkempt mustache.

Now, just thinking about it made Director Shi's eyes blaze. That little devil had returned to Wujia Town in a spirited manner, having made a fortune with two big trunks, inviting Lian Lao and Old Mu to dinner, but Director Shi didn't care about those! But that little devil was of unknown origin. Director Shi forgot to eat his shaobing and wasn't it because he was in "deep thought and long-range planning?" His brain naturally started working, and his inch-long fingernails were knocking on the table, but out of rhythm, like a sparrow's nest being knocked over, chirping and chirping.

Lian Lao couldn't guess whether the Director was having a poetic inspiration or if he had blurted something out? He gave Old Mu a look of exasperation, elegantly picked up his teacup, and took a sip of scalding tea, finally putting the heart that was about to jump out back in its nest.

A Geng originally didn't understand those "airs," and now he was slapping the back of his head as if he were standing in front of a funhouse mirror. There must be something wrong in his head, he muttered.

Suddenly, Director Shi slammed his palm on the table. This was a lapse in his self-control; Old Mu and Lian Lao were not like A Geng, this was a loss of "decency." They hurriedly put on smiling faces and said,

"This is the virtue accumulated by the Lu family ancestors, and also the happiness of Wujia Town!" He struck the table twice in a row as if to express his satisfaction. "That child has become good, I'll have to go see him in a while."

That way, the two gentlemen, acting like influenza patients, chimed in and hit the table.

Director Shi is Director Shi for a reason; this trick is beyond others.

The conversation came to an end, and the "connection" that A Geng couldn't figure out also came to an end in Director Shi's belly. "Could Be Currie" temporarily fell silent. The three scholars sipped their tea elegantly. A Geng was watching Old Mu and Lian Lao's mouths, thinking that if the two of them couldn't taste the tea, he could put even less "Daidaihua" tea in tomorrow. Turning to the idea of making money, A Geng's head wasn't wired wrong.

The sun shone into the threshold and then retreated. A Geng raised his hand to the street and estimated that it was already past five o'clock. He still remembered that Master Shi hadn't eaten his shaobing. Now that he had the chance, he coughed, this was not "airs," he wanted to attract Master Shi's attention, pulling out an touching tone, he called: "A Geng, make two shaobing!"

But Master Shi's attention was completely focused on that unfathomable "connection." Although he had slammed the table and tapped his fingernails, as a Director in "deep thought and long-range planning," he had to "think thrice before acting." That child's origins were unknown, and before he went to "take a look at him," he couldn't remember that he hadn't eaten his shaobing today.

(Note 1) Rural teahouses provide water pipes for customers to use. The tobacco is stored in a specially made iron box, one box being called a "装."

(Note 2) "Qing Tiao" is a type of tobacco.

Seaside Tragedy (Part 2)

— Original by Honoré de Balzac, translated by Wang Jiansheng

Despite the sweltering heat of the day and the fatigue of the long journey, our souls were still filled with an indescribable, fervent joy. They overflowed with a pure happiness, a joy akin to that felt when listening to a melodious piece of music, such as Mozart's renowned "Let us go, my love." Two souls merged into a single, pure thought, just as two beautiful voices harmonize in a song.

To fully understand the emotions that later assailed us, you must share the unconstrained joy we experienced during our morning stroll. If you were to sit for a moment and observe a wild dove perched on a branch overhanging a small stream, its vibrant colors a mix of hues, only to see it seized by an eagle's iron talons, snatched away as swiftly as a bullet propelled from a gun, you would tremble and lose your voice in sorrow.

We soon reached a small stone cave, in front of which stood a narrow reef, rising a hundred feet above the sea. Further ahead, a sheer cliff of rock blocked the surging waves. As we climbed two steps onto the stone platform, we felt an electric-like tremor pass through our bodies, akin to being startled by a sudden, loud sound in the middle of the night.

I caught sight of a man sitting on a rock, staring at us.

The gaze he shot from his bloodshot eye sockets was like the flash of a cannon. His limbs were numb and motionless, as if he were one of the granite pillars amidst which he sat. His body remained rigid, as if he had turned to stone, with only his eyes moving slowly. After he glanced at us, deeply moving us, he shifted his gaze away, focusing on the vast ocean stretching before his feet. Despite the intense light glinting off the water's surface, he stared intently, as an eagle is said to gaze at the sun. He did not raise his eyes again. My dear uncle, imagine the withered top of an oak tree, stripped bare by time, its gnarled trunk still displaying its unique shape beside a desolate road—that is a true likeness of this man. His torso resembled that of a ruined Hercules, his face, like a suffering god of Olympus, scarred by age, coarse food, and the hardships of sea life, as if scorched by an electric current. I gazed at his rough, hairy hands, and I saw muscles like iron chains. Throughout his entire being, the signs of similarly endowed strength were revealed.

In a corner of the small stone cave, I glimpsed a large pile of moss and a rough stone shelf, naturally formed on the granite surface. On the shelf sat an earthenware pot, scattered with crumbs of round bread. My imagination—even when wandering among the hermits of the early Christian faith dwelling in the desert—had never conjured up such a sublime religious image, or such a terrible scene of penance. Surely you, my dear uncle, with your experience of penitents, have never seen such a noble heart in penance, a heart immersed in the waves of prayer, in the eternal silence of desperate prayer.

Shen Bao Local Supplement

Commentary

Usurious Loans

— Chen

The other day, a newspaper reported that Xu Zhenyin, a Yancheng native living in a shack in western Shanghai, supported himself by pulling a rickshaw. His wife, Chen, finding her husband's meager earnings insufficient to make ends meet, secretly borrowed usurious loans from Chen Hongming. Now, Chen is pressuring her for repayment. Unable to repay the debt, she became despondent and, behind everyone's back, hanged herself. When her husband discovered her, she was already lifeless and cold. He immediately reported the matter to the police station, who summoned Chen Hongming for questioning.

Originally, there are a number of suicides due to economic pressure every day. There are many publicized relief methods in society. Regardless of the outcome, it is unnecessary to discuss too much. However, in this case, the deceased essentially died at the hands of the usurious lender. Here, I would like to briefly elaborate on the matter of "usurious loans." In my hometown, there is a popular folk song that roughly goes, "High-interest loans, usurious money, are man-eating beasts, swords that kill." These few words are enough to demonstrate the misery and severity caused by usurious loans. Many people have been forced to their deaths under this "super-economic exploitation." I remember that the government once explicitly prohibited "usurious loans," as they were no different from plunder. However, those who prohibit do so, while those struggling in hunger and cold still have no choice but to try this poisonous, bitter medicine.

Even in Shanghai, despite its thriving commerce and industry, usurious loans continue to grow insidiously in shantytowns and slums. Typically, borrowing one yuan at usurious interest incurs a daily interest of at least three copper coins, and often up to five, and the interest must be paid daily, otherwise, interest is added to interest. Calculated this way, the annual interest can be more than two or three times the principal. This kind of extortion is truly too cruel. To repay the loan, it is already difficult to even fill one's stomach, so how can one have the ability to repay it at once? Not repaying it, however, means that this long-term heavy interest is simply sucking the marrow from one's bones. Moreover, with constant hunger and cold pressing in, one must borrow new debts, and the debts accumulate more and more. The more debts there are, the more difficult it becomes to repay them. As a result, many people teetering on the brink of starvation become long-term beasts of burden for the bloodsuckers who lend usurious money. This inhuman plunder truly plays out countless tragedies and creates countless evils. The self-hanging of Xu and Chen is merely one of countless victims of usurious exploitation.

Dusk

— Fang Yin

I love dusk. However, as dusk approaches, I become somewhat afraid. I fear its fleeting existence, I fear the night demon that follows it, I fear, I fear! I fear it symbolizes the setting of the sun, the arrival of an end.

A streak of crimson sunset hangs on the edge of the sky, and I suspect that it may hide pools of blood—not the blood of beasts, but the blood of lambs!—This blood records the brutal battles of the day.

I dream that perhaps some light will be revealed in the sunset clouds, but it will not be in the evening, but in the dawn that slowly slips out of the net of darkness after a night of battle.

Now, dusk is approaching again. I look at the sky, gazing at the fiery red sun sinking in the west.

Crows and sparrows noisily return to their nests in the forest, and the pastoral songs of the fields are scattered with melancholy. It is the return of flocks of sheep and oxen, a sigh after a day of fatigue.

Looking at the sky, looking at the dry fields, countless tanned faces are full of sorrow, countless backs weighed down by heavy burdens, and it is clear that not a trace of electricity can be found from the horizon...

The chimney stops its black smoke, and people with sallow faces drag their tired legs out of the factory, like flocks of sheep and oxen returning. A large group, a large group of people who spend all day suffocating under gas. They don't see the sun all day, only catching a glimpse of the sunset glow at dusk. However, before the fatigue of the day has even recovered, hearing the whistle howl like the call of a wolf, people must struggle stubbornly out of the darkness and crawl into that bottomless cave before dawn. …

I fear dusk, I love dusk, … I hope to find some tranquility in dusk, to breathe a sigh of relief before the dark night. …

But, in the corners of the city, linger that group; the crowds who never care whether it is dusk, night, or day? They reach out a dirty hand to beg for the mercy of others, using thousands of compliments to beg for scraps of food, hoping that the strength they can't sell and a small life can be temporarily prolonged. They constantly beg, regardless of day, night, or dusk, and then sleep in some indefinite street corner, clutching an emaciated stomach.

There is no tranquility here, but endless wailing and groaning!

I can't bear to hear the cries of mourning. I am horrified by the crimson sunset, and I fear such dusk. But, I need to find a moment of tranquility in between.

I once walked from the distant desert to the edge of the sea with a wounded heart. I begged like a beggar, prolonging this half-dead life. I understand myself better than anyone else, I understand that I need to survive.

The devil grabs me with his ferocious claws, and he wants to drive me behind those who die old and die young, urging me to fall from dusk into the black night like a dead city.

However, I don't want to sink, I don't want to die, I still have to struggle, from this dusk to that dusk. I know: before dusk is day, and after night is dawn. But, but my age has not yet reached the time of twilight, I have to break free from this claw, and with a wounded body, pass one dusk after another,

Yes, I once received a gentle caress in the dusk, a sea breeze awakened me from my coma, but as soon as the dusk disappeared, the night demon came to hunt me down again. I will continue to seize the second dawn

The evening breeze lifted the corners of my clothes, and the sun gradually sank between the mountains. I chased, I ran, I sprinted, I wanted to save the swallowed sun. But I fell in the mountain col, I did not retrieve our sun, I was injured, and slowly dragged my injured body, and climbed up again in the rugged mountains.

From then on, I was even more afraid of dusk like this.

However, when I am so tired from daytime work that I feel as weak as if I am dying, I feel that dusk is astringent, tranquil, and lovely. Therefore, I have to love dusk, but I am also afraid of dusk...

Painting Interests

— Hua Mu

There are two ways to look at paintings. Small pieces such as cartoons and title page illustrations can only be enjoyed in private, early in the morning or late at night, or when waking up before bed. In addition to looking at these kinds of small paintings, there is also the fun of reading them. They are not just for sensory satisfaction. Large hanging scrolls, especially the screen paintings in Chinese painting, can only be hung in a splendidly decorated reception room to achieve harmony in mood. But these things are only for viewing and playing, and there is no fun in reading them. Although the ancients once praised Wang Wei's paintings as having poetry in them, this was perhaps because Wang Wei's paintings were in harmony with the life mood of people in that era. In the modern era of steamboats, trains, and Western suits and leather shoes, that kind of ancient life mood is of course difficult to exist in our lives.

However, the style of Chinese paintings created by contemporary painters is completely different from modern life. The background must be mountains and forests, misty rain, flowing water and small bridges, and the characters must be high-ranking, wide-robed reclusive farmers and woodcutters. The ladies must have slender heads and moth eyebrows, with narrow shoulders and slender waists. It is truly strange to the extreme that modern people create paintings with ancient moods. Some people say that the imitative nature of Chinese people is higher than their creative nature, or is this what it means? If someone were to say that it is precisely this that can express the spirit and thoughts of Eastern people, then one can't help but feel "unbelievable."

Speaking of this, I can't help but praise Mr. Feng Zikai's paintings. He uses Chinese brushstrokes, lines, and emotions to depict the life mood of modern Chinese people. Although only

Page 18

they are small pieces, their artistic expressive function is not comparable to those "Chinese paintings."

Going to art exhibitions held by groups, organizations, or individuals, I also feel that it is a kind of murder of my emotions. Seeing some swollen-looking human figures, I feel a burst of discomfort in my heart. The performance of art certainly has elements of exaggeration and emphasis, but it cannot be excessive. Although these figure paintings come from the West, I can't bear to deceive myself and say that these are beautiful works of art. The price tag in one corner of the painting is also quite difficult to look at. Since they want to fill their stomachs with paintings, they should just say that they are selling paintings. Why must they beautify it by calling it an art exhibition? I am not ridiculing that it is a shameful and lowly thing for painters to sell paintings for money. The modern way of life is originally trending towards the extremes of commodification and complexity. This cannot but make people think of the "painting beggars" on the streets of Paris. They have nothing outside their bodies, and only use a few crude chalk pencils to write out their works on the smooth road, asking passers-by for some alms. Although this way of selling paintings is somewhat pitiful, their straightforwardness and unrestrainedness are worthy of admiration.

Playing the zither, playing chess, calligraphy, and painting were everyday activities for scholars of previous generations, and those who could not play the zither, play chess, write, or paint could never be ranked among talented and elegant people. As for using these pastimes to advance in society, there is naturally another way of saying it. Looking at contemporary scholar-painters, how many can write and paint, and paint and write? However, this cannot be said to be evidence that modern people are inferior to people in the past. On the contrary, from a scientific point of view, it is a progressive expression of modern art. Mechanical civilization has given rise to a social mode of production that must adopt a division of labor; and contemporary literary painters can naturally only devote themselves to their respective arts for the rest of their lives. And to say that the productions of painters who cannot write are far inferior to the ancients is absolutely untenable. Naturally, the Chinese people's emphasis on the ancient and disregard for the modern is not unique to art. The ancient must be good, and the modern must be bad. It has become the criterion for criticism. How many collectors are arrogant and unyielding, and the value of ancient paintings and engravings is incalculable. However, I believe that there are few who value their precious antiques entirely for the sake of artistic interest, or who attempt to extract the social significance contained in ancient paintings and engravings. What is the pleasure of their craze for ancient paintings? It is because these are things from previous generations. That contemporary Chinese painters must painstakingly take ancient methods as the standard, in addition to their lack of creativity and strength, this may be one of the reasons.

Some people say that art in the future will inevitably return to the hands of the masses and become something of their own. This statement is certainly not wrong, but I think this may also be a matter for future generations. The lives, education, and ideals of the masses are still far apart. The masses of today can never find their own interests in swollen-looking human figure paintings and paintings of reclusive scholars in mountains and forests; and artists with artistic education will never bother to appreciate comic strips such as Hundred Beauties Pictures or Swordsmen Tales. What the public needs are things like this! Theorists who shout high-pitched theories are actually a bit too anxious.

The current popularity of cartoon production cannot be said to be not because it can capture the interests of a part of the public. This phenomenon is worth rejoicing. Using Chinese brushstrokes, emotions, and lines to depict the life mood of modern Chinese people is what we hope our painters will work hard to do.

Spending the Night in a Desolate Village

— Xin Ye

I had no planned itinerary for this journey, but due to an urgent matter, I was heading to Changsha. However, upon inquiring, there were no convenient boats heading east lately, so I boarded a gravel boat headed for Changsha.

Riding a gravel boat also has a tradition: as long as you are from the same hometown, they not only absolutely won't charge you for the boat fare and drinks, but they will also provide meals for the passengers. However, they don't run boats very often, so it is very rare to catch their boat.

In the morning breeze, carrying my only suitcase! And bag, I hurriedly got off the boat, and the boat slowly left the wharf. Because the hull was too heavy, the voyage was very slow. As someone with worries on my mind, I felt really anxious sitting inside, but what was the use of being anxious? The boat won't move faster. There was no other way, so I had to look at the scenery of the lake or chat with the people on the boat; in this way, my uneasy heart calmed down.

"Today the wind is a favorable wind, but the weather is not clear," the boat owner squatted by the helm, clamped down on the rudder, one hand resting on the oar stern, saying very serenely.

"I'm afraid it will be after dusk when we arrive at Weisha!" A shop assistant leaned against the mast, watching the milky white gossamer flying in the sky.

Lunch was served on the boat. The meal on the boat was extremely simple: a bowl of pickled vegetables, tofu, bean sprouts, and salted fish, but it still had a special flavor, and watching them eat so voraciously was quite interesting.

"How far is the town from the pier, and is there a place to eat there?" After finishing my meal, I suddenly thought that if the boat arrived late, I wouldn't have a place to stay. (To be continued)

National Studies

Feng Mingquan "Selections from Ancient Prose"

Ming Yuan (960) 7:30 AM to 8:30 AM

Liu Zongyuan's "Debate on the Analects" (1): Someone asked, "Scholars praise the Analects, saying it was recorded by Confucius' disciples. Is this credible?" I say, "Not so." Confucius' disciple Zeng Shen was the youngest, 46 years younger than Confucius. Zeng died in old age. This book records Zeng's death, so it is far removed from Confucius. When Zeng died, almost none of Confucius' disciples survived. I believe it was written by Zeng's disciples. Why? Moreover, this book always refers to disciples by their given names, but Zeng alone is referred to as Zi. From this, it can be said that it is not a disciple's record. Then why is You referred to as Zi? I say, "When Confucius died, the disciples regarded You as similar to the master, and they stood and took him as their teacher. Later, unable to answer the disciples' questions, he scolded them and withdrew. So, he once had the title of teacher. Now, the record only states that Zeng died last. Therefore, I know that it was written by the followers of Zichun of Lezheng, or Confucius' disciples vaguely recorded his words, but the one who eventually completed the book was Zeng's disciple.

Shen Bao Architectural Weekly

Japanese Temple Architecture (3)

— Ito Chuta (Author), Zhang Guangzheng (Translator)

Buddhist Temple Architecture

Japanese Buddhist temple architecture is completely different from shrine architecture. Shrine architecture is purely an original creation of ancient Japan; Buddhist temple architecture is the Japanization of styles transmitted from China. The form of Chinese Buddhist temple halls originally followed the model of the highly developed palace architecture of ancient China. In China, there was no special style called Buddhist temple architecture. Therefore, the root of Japanese Buddhist temple architecture is Chinese palaces.

Moreover, nearly 1,400 years have passed since Buddhism was introduced to Japan. The Japanese people, accustomed to the Chinese-style Buddhist temple architecture, gradually developed a deep sense of intimacy with it, and now have reached a level of love and cannot be separated from it. Therefore, it is only natural that current Buddhist temple architecture almost entirely follows the old model. However, from a purely theoretical perspective, Buddhist temple architecture cannot be determined to necessarily follow the current Chinese-style form. This is very different from the reason why shrines cannot but absolutely adhere to ancient forms.

Shrines are the palaces where the spirits of the Japanese imperial ancestors reside, so their architecture must be based on the style of ancient times. However, Buddhist temples are the halls where the founders of Buddhism and all the Buddhas reside, so it would not be strange to imitate India, or create unique halls and pagodas based on inspiration from Buddhist scriptures.

In recent years, the nature of Buddhist temples has gradually become that of public buildings. They are used for preaching and funerals, or for lectures on Dharma affairs, or for religious affairs and public undertakings, and for various other purposes. To adapt to this, their architecture will naturally undergo a fate of change. In short, it cannot be determined that Buddhist temple architecture will permanently maintain its ancient style. Recently, Buddhist temples of new styles are being built everywhere. We don't need to be too surprised by this.

From Architectural Art to the Temples of Greece (6)

— Sun Zongwen

—The Most Glorious Page in the History of World Architecture—

In addition to contributing their capital art to the world, the Greeks also had great research on the design of columns; they made many contributions to architectural design in later generations. Therefore, we also deserve to discuss it. Its design features have the following points:

1. The design of columns and beams is absolutely centrally substantial.

2. The arrangement of the columns is whole and integrated, not fragmented and disordered.

3. The selection of materials has special advantages; because they can know the properties of various materials and other experiences, the building can increase its durability.

4. At that time, also because of the research on the earth's gravity, the size of the building was a certain rule, especially the design of beams and columns, so there was no risk of fracture or other. This point can increase the safety of the building and is one of the greatest contributions at that time.

5. The design of beams and columns applies the law of Optical Corrections, which is described below:

Because we see an extremely long straight line, the two ends of the line always feel bent upward. And if you look at a slightly curved horizontal line, it will sink its very straightness; therefore, based on this reason, when designing a horizontal beam, the Greeks did not draw a straight line but slightly curved it. Similarly, because we always become curved when we look at a straight line, the Greeks also drew longitudinal curved lines when making straight columns; therefore, the columns expand to the left and right sides. These two points cannot be said to be a great contribution of the Greeks to architectural design. From this, we can see that the superior talent of the Greeks can be said to be fully utilized without any regret.

The Greeks also designed the surrounding columns of the temple building according to the above-mentioned principles, the principle of visual correction, so the diameters of the leftmost and rightmost two columns on the front are slightly larger, and their distance to the adjacent one and the one behind is slightly shorter; therefore, people's eyes do not feel monotonous; because the back of the column is not a wall but the sky; if the diameter of the column is not larger, then people's eyes will feel too monotonous! Similarly, according to this principle, the top of any text written on the building is the largest, and gradually shrinks downward; the reason is that when people stand in front and look up, they always feel that the words on the top are smaller than those on the bottom. In order to correct this visual error, the Greeks studied that the words on the top should be larger than those on the bottom.

Architectural History (3)

— Buren

In the Middle Ages, architectural art was developing in most European countries, and each country had its own characteristics. In England, we can roughly divide the "Gothic" period into:

Early English style – 13th century.

Decorated style – 14th century.

Perpendicular style – 15th century.

By the 16th century, "Gothic" gradually declined, and it was replaced by the architectural style called "Tudor". "Tudor style" contains the flavor of ancient Roman architecture, and thus produced the architecture of the early Renaissance.

The great Renaissance that occurred in Italy in the 15th century produced one result among many results: Italian architects carefully studied the buildings of ancient Rome, and thus designed many fresh and beautiful ancient-style new patterns. England, because of its distance, still adopted the old architectural style and was not affected by the new patterns after Italy and its neighbors had abolished "Gothic". Of course, the craze of the Renaissance finally swept to England, but this was not directly sandwiched, but came through France and the Netherlands, so the early British Renaissance buildings were mostly a strange mixture, half "Gothic" or "Tudor", half Italian, and a bit Dutch, or French, or German.

The change of style is mostly first discovered in small programs about decoration; these new designs are first tested on house decorations, metal work, colored decorations, paintings, and things like music stands and tombs, and then are adopted by practical buildings. The change is slow and uneven. Sometimes it takes a long time to reach remote areas, as long as fifty years.

During the reign of Elizabeth and James I, the so-called early Renaissance architectural style was prevalent throughout England. After the famous architect Inigo Jones returned from studying Italian buildings to the court of Charles I, English architecture began to lean towards a new style that was relatively unmixed and more closely resembled the works of the Romans and their skilled descendants. Jones certainly made many patterns and designs, but due to the obstruction of the civil war, not many buildings were actually built. His most famous work—the Banquet Hall in "Whitehall"—is only a small part of the grand palace he intended to build for Charles I.

The second famous British architect was Sir Christopher Wren. He had never been to Italy, but when he went to Paris to visit Louis XVI's newly built palace, he met many Italian and French architects.

What Wren brought back were certainly Italian and French architectural plans, but these plans all contained his own various creative plans. He was indeed called the creator of "Anglo-Classic Style" architecture. The reconstruction of London houses after the "Great Fire" gave him a chance to show his skills. "St. Paul's" and many other local churches still exist today, which are symbols of his talent. He also built Hampton Court, Greenwich Hospital, Chelsea Hospital, and many beautiful private residences, both in towns and in the countryside. In fact, in the history of British architecture, Wren's achievements are greater than any other architect, whether before or after; and, if the houses in London had been completely rebuilt according to his plan after the "Great Fire", then the splendor of the British capital could have defeated the world.

After Wren, straight through the 18th century, England produced many good architects, each with their own slightly special plans, but all of them were based on the great classical style that Wren first created and anglicized.

In the early 19th century, the new discoveries, new identifications, and new collapses of the ruins of ancient Greece and ancient Rome made people's thoughts return to the true origins of classical architecture, and the result was "Classical Revleal". The buildings produced from this—such as St Geotoes Hallid in Liverpool—are sufficiently noble and magnificent, but not entirely suitable for the interests of the British people.
